8/6/23, 10:32 - PPS: A placement cell is a department in an educational institution that is responsible for facilitating the recruitment of students by companies. The placement cell typically works with companies to identify suitable candidates, and then coordinates the interview process and job offers. The placement cell also provides support to students in preparing for interviews and negotiating job offers.

Here are some of the key functions of a placement cell:

* **Identifying potential employers:** The placement cell will typically maintain a database of companies that are interested in recruiting students from the institution. They will also reach out to new companies to encourage them to participate in the placement process.
* **Screening candidates:** The placement cell will screen candidates to ensure that they meet the minimum requirements of the employers. This may involve reviewing resumes, conducting interviews, and administering tests.
* **Coordinating the interview process:** The placement cell will coordinate the interview process between the students and the employers. This may involve scheduling interviews, arranging transportation, and providing support to students.
* **Providing support to students:** The placement cell will provide support to students in preparing for interviews and negotiating job offers. This may involve providing mock interviews, coaching on resume writing, and helping students to understand the job market.

The placement cell plays an important role in helping students to secure good jobs after graduation. By providing support and guidance, the placement cell can help students to make the most of their education and achieve their career goals.

Here are some of the benefits of a placement cell for students:

* **Increased chances of getting a job:** The placement cell has a network of companies that are interested in recruiting students from the institution. This gives students a better chance of getting a job after graduation.
* **Access to better job opportunities:** The placement cell can help students to identify and apply for jobs that they may not have been aware of otherwise. They can also help students to negotiate better salaries and benefits.
* **Improved job search skills:** The placement cell can provide students with training on how to search for jobs, prepare for interviews, and negotiate job offers. This can help students to improve their job search skills and increase their chances of getting a good job.
* **Reduced stress:** The placement cell can take care of the job search process for students. This can help students to reduce stress and focus on their studies.

If you are a student, I encourage you to get involved with your institution's placement cell. The placement cell can be a valuable resource for you in your job search.

Explore how machine learning can help healthcare providers identify patients at risk of readmission and implement preventive measures.

Energy Consumption Forecasting: Utilize RapidMiner to analyze historical energy consumption data and build a model that predicts energy demand in different scenarios. This can have applications in energy management and resource allocation.

Market Basket Analysis for Retail Optimization: Use RapidMiner to perform market basket analysis on transaction data from a retail store. Discover associations between items that are frequently purchased together to optimize product placement and marketing strategies.

Image Classification and Recognition: Explore image classification tasks using RapidMiner's capabilities. Develop a model to classify images into specific categories, such as identifying different species of plants, animals, or objects.

Credit Scoring and Risk Assessment: Create a credit scoring model in RapidMiner that assesses the creditworthiness of individuals based on various financial and personal attributes. Investigate how different data preprocessing techniques and algorithms impact the model's accuracy.

Supply Chain Optimization: Investigate how RapidMiner can be used to optimize supply chain processes. Develop a model to predict demand, optimize inventory levels, or streamline distribution routes.

10/5/23, 14:55 - PPS: Job Satisfaction: The level of satisfaction with one's job can significantly influence mental health. A fulfilling and meaningful job can contribute positively to mental well-being, while a job that is unsatisfying or stressful may have adverse effects.

Financial Stability: Employment is often tied to financial stability. Economic stressors, such as unemployment or financial insecurity, can contribute to mental health issues like anxiety and depression.

Social Connection: Workplaces provide opportunities for social interaction and a sense of belonging. Lack of social connection, especially in the context of unemployment, can contribute to feelings of isolation and negatively impact mental health.

Routine and Structure: Employment often provides a daily routine and structure. Predictability and routine can be beneficial for mental health, helping individuals maintain a sense of stability and purpose.

Workplace Stress: The nature of the work environment and the level of stress it imposes can impact mental health. High-stress jobs without proper support mechanisms may contribute to mental health challenges.

10/5/23, 15:24 - Srisha🌝: Here are some attributes that you can use to create a dataset for mental health prediction:

Demographic attributes: Age, gender, race/ethnicity, socioeconomic status, education level, marital status, employment status, etc.
Medical history: Personal and family history of mental illness, physical health conditions, medications taken, etc.
Lifestyle factors: Diet, exercise, sleep habits, substance use, social support, etc.
Cognitive factors: Thinking patterns, memory, attention, problem-solving skills, etc.
Emotional factors: Mood, anxiety, stress, anger, etc.
Behavioral factors: Sleep problems, changes in appetite, social withdrawal, self-harm, etc.
In addition to these general attributes, you may also want to include more specific attributes that are relevant to the mental health conditions that you are interested in predicting. For example, if you are interested in predicting depression, you may want to include attributes such as feelings of hopelessness, guilt, worthlessness, and loss of interest in activities that were once enjoyable.

You can collect these attributes from a variety of sources, such as surveys, interviews, medical records, and wearable devices. It is important to note that some of these attributes may be sensitive, so it is important to obtain consent from participants before collecting any data.

Here are some examples of specific attributes that you could use to create a dataset for predicting depression:

Beck Depression Inventory (BDI) score: The BDI is a self-report questionnaire that is used to assess the severity of depressive symptoms.
Patient Health Questionnaire (PHQ-9) score: The PHQ-9 is another self-report questionnaire that is used to assess depressive symptoms.
Generalized Anxiety Disorder-7 (GAD-7) score: The GAD-7 is a self-report questionnaire that is used to assess the severity of anxiety symptoms.
Social support network: This could include the number of close friends and family members that a person has, and how often they interact with them.
Sleep quality: This could include the number of hours of sleep that a person gets per night, and how often they have trouble falling asleep or staying asleep.
Exercise habits: This could include the frequency and intensity of exercise that a person engages in.
Substance use: This could include the type and amount of alcohol or drugs that a person uses.
By collecting a dataset that includes a variety of attributes, you will be able to develop a more accurate and informative mental health prediction model.

Necessary attributes for prediction of students mental health 

The following attributes are important to consider for predicting students' mental health:

Academic factors: GPA, attendance, grades in individual subjects, test scores, etc.
Behavioral factors: Tardiness, absenteeism, disruptive behavior, disciplinary actions, etc.
Emotional factors: Mood swings, anxiety, stress, irritability, etc.
Social factors: Social support network, peer relationships, family relationships, etc.
Lifestyle factors: Sleep quality, exercise habits, diet, substance use, etc.
Medical history: Personal and family history of mental illness, physical health conditions, medications taken, etc.
In addition to these general attributes, you may also want to include more specific attributes that are relevant to the mental health conditions that you are interested in predicting. For example, if you are interested in predicting depression, you may want to include attributes such as feelings of hopelessness, guilt, worthlessness, and loss of interest in activities that were once enjoyable.

You can collect these attributes from a variety of sources, such as surveys, interviews, student records, and teacher feedback. It is important to note that some of these attributes may be sensitive, so it is important to obtain consent from students or their parents before collecting any data.

Here are some examples of specific attributes that you could use to create a dataset for predicting student depression:

Beck Depression Inventory - Student (BDI-II) score: The BDI-II is a self-report questionnaire that is used to assess the severity of depressive symptoms in adolescents.
Child Depression Inventory (CDI-2) score: The CDI-2 is another self-report questionnaire that is used to assess depressive symptoms in children and adolescents.
Screen for Child Anxiety Related Emotional Disorders (SCARED) score: The SCARED is a self-report questionnaire that is used to assess anxiety symptoms in children and adolescents.
Social support network: This could include the number of close friends and family members that a student has, and how often they interact with them.
Sleep quality: This could include the number of hours of sleep that a student gets per night, and how often they have trouble falling asleep or staying asleep.
Exercise habits: This could include the frequency and intensity of exercise that a student engages in.
Substance use: This could include the type and amount of alcohol or drugs that a student uses.
By collecting a dataset that includes a variety of attributes, you will be able to develop a more accurate and informative student mental health prediction model. This information can be used to identify students who are at risk for developing mental health problems, and to provide them with the support and resources that they need.

Symptoms of people suffering with mental health issues 
View other drafts



















Symptoms of mental health issues can vary depending on the specific condition. However, some common symptoms include:

Changes in mood: Feeling sad, anxious, or irritable for long periods of time.
Changes in thinking: Having trouble concentrating, making decisions, or remembering things.
Changes in behavior: Withdrawing from social activities, having trouble sleeping, or eating too much or too little.
Changes in physical health: Having headaches, stomachaches, or other physical problems that can't be explained by a medical condition.
If you are experiencing any of these symptoms, it is important to talk to a doctor or mental health professional. They can help you to understand what you are going through and develop a treatment plan that is right for you.

Here are some specific examples of symptoms for common mental health conditions:

Depression: Feeling sad or hopeless most of the time, loss of interest in activities that were once enjoyable, changes in appetite or sleep, difficulty concentrating, thoughts of death or suicide.
Anxiety: Feeling nervous, restless, or tense most of the time, having a sense of impending danger, having trouble sleeping, avoiding situations that trigger anxiety.
Bipolar disorder: Extreme mood swings, alternating between periods of mania (high energy and activity) and depression.
Schizophrenia: Hallucinations (seeing or hearing things that aren't there), delusions (false beliefs), disorganized thinking and speech.
Post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD): Flashbacks of a traumatic event, nightmares, trouble sleeping, avoiding reminders of the event, feeling on edge or jittery.

10/13/23, 21:55 - Anu: class Person:
    def __init__(self, firstname, familyname, birthfamilyname, gender, fathername, spousename, age):
        self.firstname = firstname
        self.familyname = familyname
        self.birthfamilyname = birthfamilyname
        self.gender = gender
        self.fathername = fathername
        self.spousename = spousename
        self.age = age

    def __str__(self):
        return f"{self.firstname} {self.familyname} {self.birthfamilyname} {self.gender} {self.fathername} {self.spousename} {self.age}"

    def is_alive(self):
        return not hasattr(self, 'death_year')

    def set_leader(self, leader):
        self.leader = leader

def find_leader(family):
    if len(family) == 0:
        return None
    leader = family[0]
    for person in family:
        if person.age > leader.age:
            leader = person
        elif person.age == leader.age:
            if person.familyname < leader.familyname:
                leader = person
    for person in family:
        person.set_leader(leader)

def find_family(person, all_families):
    for family in all_families:
        if person in family:
            return family
    return None

N = int(input())
persons = {}
all_families = []

for _ in range(N):
    data = input().split()
    person = Person(*data)
    persons[data[0]] = person
    family = find_family(person, all_families)
    if family is None:
        all_families.append([person])
    else:
        family.append(person)

E = int(input())

for _ in range(E):
    event = input().split()
    if event[0] == "MA":
        person1 = persons[event[1]]
        person2 = persons[event[2]]
        family1 = find_family(person1, all_families)
        family2 = find_family(person2, all_families)
        if family1 != family2:
            family1.extend(family2)
            all_families.remove(family2)
        find_leader(family1)
    elif event[0] == "DI":
        person1 = persons[event[1]]
        person2 = persons[event[2]]
        family1 = find_family(person1, all_families)
        family2 = find_family(person2, all_families)
        if person1.familyname != person2.birthfamilyname:
            continue
        if family1 == family2:
            family1.remove(person1)
            family2.remove(person2)
            find_leader(family1)
            find_leader(family2)
        if not person1.is_alive():
            person2.birthfamilyname = person1.birthfamilyname
            find_leader(family1)
        else:
            person1.familyname = person2.familyname
            family2.append(person1)
            family1.remove(person1)
            find_leader(family1)
            find_leader(family2)
    elif event[0] == "BI":
        new_person = Person(*event[1:])
        persons[new_person.firstname] = new_person
        family = find_family(new_person, all_families)
        family.append(new_person)
        find_leader(family)
    elif event[0] == "DE":
        person = persons[event[1]]
        person.death_year = int(event[2])

PA_output = []
for family in all_families:
    for person in family:
        if person.is_alive():
            PA_output.append(person)
PA_output.sort(key=lambda x: x.firstname)

for person in PA_output:
    print(person)

10/13/23, 21:55 - Anu: def allocate_college_seats(C, N, college_seats, students): 
    college_students = {f'C-{i}': [] for i in range(1, C + 1)}
    for student_id, percentage, choice1, choice2, choice3 in students:
        # Create a list of tuples with (college, choice) pairs
        choices = [(choice1, 1), (choice2, 2), (choice3, 3)]
        # Sort choices based on preference
        choices.sort(key=lambda x: x[1])
        for choice, pref in choices:
            if college_seats[choice] > 0:
                college_students[choice].append((student_id, percentage))
                college_seats[choice] -= 1
                break
    for college, student_list in college_students.items():
        student_list.sort(key=lambda x: (-x[1], x[0]))
    for student_id, percentage, choice1, choice2, choice3 in students:
        if student_id not in {student[0] for students in college_students.values()}:
            available_colleges = [choice1, choice2, choice3]
            for choice in available_colleges:
                if college_seats[choice] > 0:
                    college_students[choice].append((student_id, percentage))
                    college_seats[choice] -= 1
                    break
    return college_students
C, N = map(int, input().split())
college_seats = {}
for seats in input().split():
    college, seats = seats.split('-')
    college_seats[f'C-{college}'] = int(seats)
students = []
for _ in range(N):
    student_info = input().split(',')
    student_id = student_info[0]
    percentage = float(student_info[1])
    choices = student_info[2:]
    students.append((student_id, percentage, *choices))
college_allocation = allocate_college_seats(C, N, college_seats, students)
cut_offs = []
for college, student_list in college_allocation.items():
    if student_list:
        cut_off_percentage = student_list[-1][1]
        cut_offs.append((college, cut_off_percentage))
cut_offs.sort(key=lambda x: (-x[1], x[0]))
for college, cut_off_percentage in cut_offs:
    print(f'{college} {cut_off_percentage:.2f}')
for college in college_allocation.keys():
    if not college_allocation[college]:
        print(f'{college} n/a')

10/13/23, 21:55 - Anu: def allocate_tasks(N, M, tasks):
    workers = [(f'W{i}', 0) for i in range(1, N + 1)]
    completed_tasks = []
    for task in tasks:
        task_name, task_type, completion_time = task
        if task_type == "Machine":
            worker = min(workers, key=lambda x: (x[1], x[0]))
            worker_name, worker_availability = worker
            completed_tasks.append((worker_name, task_name, worker_availability))
            index = workers.index(worker)
            workers[index] = (worker_name, worker_availability + completion_time)
        else:
            worker = min(workers, key=lambda x: (x[1], x[0]))
            worker_name, worker_availability = worker
            completed_tasks.append((worker_name, task_name, worker_availability))
            index = workers.index(worker)
            workers[index] = (worker_name, worker_availability + completion_time)
    completed_tasks.sort(key=lambda x: (x[2], x[1]))

    return completed_tasks
N = int(input())
M = int(input())
tasks = []
for _ in range(M):
    task_info = input().split()
    task_name, task_type, completion_time = task_info[0], task_info[1], int(task_info[2])
    tasks.append((task_name, task_type, completion_time))
completed_tasks = allocate_tasks(N, M, tasks)
for task in completed_tasks:
    print(f"{task[0]} {task[1]} {task[2]}")

10/13/23, 21:55 - Anu: def min_cups_to_fill_jug(N, cups, L):
    cups.sort()
    dp = [float('inf')] * (L + 1)
    dp[L] = 0
    for cup_capacity in cups:
        for j in range(cup_capacity, L + 1):
            dp[j] = min(dp[j], dp[j - cup_capacity] + 1)
    frequencies = [0] * N
    capacity_remaining = L
    for i in range(N - 1, -1, -1):
        while capacity_remaining >= cups[i]:
            if dp[capacity_remaining] == dp[capacity_remaining - cups[i]] + 1:
                frequencies[i] += 1
                capacity_remaining -= cups[i]
            else:
                break
    return cups, frequencies
N = int(input())
cups = list(map(int, input().split()))
L = int(input())
cup_sizes, frequencies = min_cups_to_fill_jug(N, cups, L)
print(" ".join(map(str, cup_sizes)))
print(" ".join(map(str, frequencies))

10/13/23, 21:55 - Anu: def get_coordinates(num, board):
    for i in range(10):
        for j in range(10):
            if board[i][j] == num:
                return i, j

def simulate_game(board, die_inputs):
    current_position = get_coordinates(1, board)
    snakes_encountered = 0
    ladders_encountered = 0

    for die_input in die_inputs:
        next_position = get_coordinates(current_position[0] - die_input, board)

        if board[next_position[0]][next_position[1]].startswith('S('):
            # Snake encountered
            snake_pos = int(board[next_position[0]][next_position[1]][2:-1])
            snakes_encountered += 1
            next_position = get_coordinates(snake_pos, board)
        elif board[next_position[0]][next_position[1]].startswith('L('):
            # Ladder encountered
            ladder_pos = int(board[next_position[0]][next_position[1]][2:-1])
            ladders_encountered += 1
            next_position = get_coordinates(ladder_pos, board)

        current_position = next_position

        if current_position == get_coordinates(End, board):
            return "Possible", snakes_encountered, ladders_encountered

    return "Not possible", snakes_encountered, ladders_encountered, board[current_position[0]][current_position[1]]

# Read input
End = 100
board = []
for _ in range(10):
    row = input().split()
    board.append(row)

die_inputs = list(map(int, input().split()))

result, snakes, ladders, final_position = simulate_game(board, die_inputs)
if result == "Not possible":
    print(f"Not possible {snakes + ladders} {final_position}")
else:
    print(f"Possible {snakes + ladders}")

10/13/23, 21:55 - Anu: def is_prime(num):
    if num <= 1:
        return False
    for i in range(2, int(num**0.5) + 1):
        if num % i == 0:
            return False
    return True

def find_smallest_prime(input_numbers):
    q = min(input_numbers)
    for num in range(q, 10**10):
        if all((num % n == q) for n in input_numbers if n != q) and is_prime(num):
            return num
    return "None"

# Read input
input_numbers = list(map(int, input().split()))

# Call the function and print the result
result = find_smallest_prime(input_numbers)
print(result)

10/27/23, 10:11 - Srisha🌝: The Principal/Dean/Director
AICTE Approved institutions

Gentle Reminder! 
Greetings from AICTE !

As you are already aware that Yuva Sangam is Yuva Sangam is a flagship program of the Ek Bharat Shreshtha Bharat (EBSB) initiative, launched by the Hon'ble Prime Minister of India. This initiative focuses on conducting exposure tours of the youths comprising mainly students studying in Higher Educational institutions (HEIs) & some off-campus youths from one state to other states & vice versa. It provides an immersive experience of various facets of life, development landmarks, recent achievements and a youth connect in the host state. The program aims to promote national integration and understanding by connecting youth from different parts of the country through educational and cultural exchanges.
 
In addition to the cultural exchange, Yuva Sangam also includes a number of other activities that are designed to help students develop their leadership, sports and soft skills. For example, students may get an opportunity not only to participate in workshops on leadership, communication, and teamwork but also sports events that may be organised by SAI. They will also have the opportunity to meet and interact with VVIPs, celebrities or important personalities of the host state.

The success of Yuva Sangam Phase 1 and 2 is a testament to the importance of investing in youth and building bridges between different communities. The program has had a positive impact on the lives of thousands of youth and has helped to build a more united and inclusive India.

I request your support in motivating students of your institution to register for Yuva Sangam Phase-3, a student exchange program under EBSB.

All interested students can register online at https://ebsb.aicte-india.org/ 

The registration process has started on 18-10-2023 and the last date is 28-10-2023.

Please find attached a Concept note containing a brief about Yuva Sangam phase 1 and 2, eligibility criteria and also a document on how to inspire students to register for Yuva Sangam.

PFA: https://docs.google.com/document/d/1qI91lYLLX85LaMuDRyai52bY1FOosbHx/edit?usp=sharing&ouid=117518947182740027481&rtpof=true&sd=true 

PFA: https://docs.google.com/document/d/1NCp7lT7aXvCf-muAjIrQ1I3Ez5PTT9yG/edit?usp=sharing&ouid=117518947182740027481&rtpof=true&sd=true 

PFA: https://docs.google.com/document/d/11GcK6ADRZXfpdyWtVNXRH5WC33aXVZ9F/edit?usp=sharing&ouid=117518947182740027481&rtpof=true&sd=true 

Thank You
(Ravindra K. Soni)
